Chapter 32.
On the Cure of Teeth Elevated above Others.
When teeth are elevated and not according to their natural form, and especially when it happens to women, and especially to venerable elderly women; therefore it is necessary that you consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth, and it is not possible to suture or bind it with ligament, then extract that one; but if it is not joined to another tooth, then cut into that one with this instrument whose form is as follows; and it is similar to the jawbone of a pig, and is made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp extremity.
And it should be your incision with which you will cut into that one over a period of several days due to the hardness of the tooth, and that it should not be like another tooth.
And if an elevated tooth is exposed to decay, then grind that one with a file of Indian iron whose form is as follows:
And it should be entirely of Indian iron and with an edge well-made; and it should be with many small points just like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is ground little by little over several days in ease, so that no other tooth becomes deformed and decayed. And then line up that one at the end, and cut off that one with some razors.
And if it is a tooth whose part is broken and irritates the tongue, then it is necessary that you grind down that one until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and equalized, and be filled so that the tongue does not irritate nor the word become corrupted.
Chapter 33.
On the Restoration of Damaged Teeth with Gold or Silver Fillings.
When accidents occur to teeth before concussion and removal through percussion or chance, and the patient cannot endure over what is left, and you have cured that one with stiptic medicines, but your cure has not been effective, then it is necessary for the reason that they do not decay to be strengthened with a gold filling and not silver, because silver becomes green and soon turns black, whereas gold is never changed.
And the filling should be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the receipt is that you take the filling and take twice its amount between two healthy teeth, and then increase it by two bends of the filling between two damaged ones, or one or several, until the fabric reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the process between the party from which you began, and strengthen your work with ease and wisdom until it is not damaged.
And then take a strong hold of the filling which you should strengthen the roots of the teeth so that they do not escape.
And then cut off the superfluous extremities of the filling with forceps or pincers, and add those to each location by gesture, and convert them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the damaged one, so that they do not cause injury to the tongue. And then let it be as straight as possible.
But if it is loose or broken, strengthen that one or those with another filling in such a way at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and figure of the restoration of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, just as these are linked:
And when one returns or two return to their places, and are rejuvenated just as we have described, and remain.
And this is done only by good artisans and wise ones.
And sometimes it is suitable for some cause of cow bones, and it is made from them as the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in a location where the tooth falls out, and it is strengthened just as we have said, and remains and serves that one for a long time.
Chapter 34.
On the Cutting of the Ligament which is found in the Tongue and prohibits Speech.
This ligament which is found behind the tongue sometimes or is natural to it which one has, or accidental and occurs by a wound which is already consolidated.
And the operation in that is that you open the mouth of the patient, and lift his tongue which exists in his own seat; and then cut into that ligament nerve according to its latitude until the tongue is released from its recent recency.
But if in that is some hardness or knotting, and this is due to the consolidation of the wound, then you must cut into it with a knife and divide it according to its latitude until you have divided the ligament and dissolved the knot.
And beware that no fissure is made in the flesh of the patient, and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the vestige of the incision with warm water or with vinegar and water, and then pause and leave the tongue to lie down; this is the way that the patient should hold his tongue in each night so that it will not consolidate again.
But if a flow of blood occurs, pause over the location with a bandage pressed; but if the blood flows out, then it is necessary to cauterize the location with a cauterizing iron which is suitable for this. And then treat that one with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the Extraction of a Ranula beneath the Tongue.
Sometimes beneath the tongue, an aposteme forms resembling a small tumor that prevents the tongue from performing its natural function; and it grows so large that it fills up the mouth.
And the operation in this is that you open the patient's mouth and examine the aposteme in the light of day, considering it carefully.
And if you see that it is a yellow or black, hard, and the patient does not feel any sensation, then do not touch it, for it is gangrenous.
And if it is whitened and has moisture, then you should place the once in it, and cut into that with a fine spatula, and cut it off completely; and if blood flows out during your operation, then pause over it with a bandage pressed, so as to stop the bleeding.
And then return to your work so that you have treated it with the complement of it, so that it is washed with vinegar and salt; and cure that one with the other cure in a similar way until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
On the Extraction of Apostemes from both Ears and that which is nothing in the Golauro and other Apostemes.
When there is a growth in the Golauro which is nothing, so that the glands which are assembled together become swollen, which swells out and are called two ears, which when you cure them with what we have said in the division and it does not heal, then you should consider if the aposteme is hard, yellow-colored, without sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red-colored and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; before that, let it be mature, and then either you will break it or it will break itself.
And if it is white-colored, rounded, and the root of it is subtle, then that one should be cut.
And the operation in this is that you consider beforehand your operation whether the aposteme has already settled or if it is diminished by some diminution; and then make the patient sit before the sun, with his head in its proper place, and open his mouth, and take hold of his tongue with an instrument very noble of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and be as subtle as a knife.
Then when you take hold of that tongue and the aposteme is shown to you, and you cut above it with the same veil, then take a once and place it in the amnella, and pull it out to the outside parts as much as you can. Or another thing that pulls some cause of the tunics, and then cut into it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Or another thing that pulls some cause of the tunics, and then cut into it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is assembled with forceps, however the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each of those is in the end of another, very sharp, made of Indian iron enveloped.
But if this instrument is not with you, then cut into it with a spatula of which this is the form of it: And be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And then, after you have cut into one notch, cut into the other one in this same manner of cutting, and then let the patient gargle with vinegar water.
But if there is bleeding, let the patient gargle with water decoction of scorsa of myrrh or fern of mirta and similar to those stiptic causes until the bleeding flux is stopped, and then cure that one until it is healed.
And sometimes there are other apostemes in the Golauro besides those two ears, and cut into them according to what I have said in the cutting of two notches equally.
And I have already treated a woman with an apostema which was born in her Golauro, which apostema tended towards yellow, without sensation, which had already closed the Golauro of hers, and she breathed with great difficulty, and had already prohibited herself from eating and drinking water, and was on the verge of death if I had not intervened within a day or two.
And the apostema had grown so much that it had eaten away two rumps and was coming out through two holes.
So I cut into it forcefully and stayed in one of them once, and pulled out that thing, and it came out from the great part, and then I cut into the other notch, and then I forced her mouth open, and grasped her tongue, and then I placed the once in that apostema.
And then I cut off a great part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. And then the Golauro of that woman was completely absolved, and she began to drink water and eat food again. And I did not cease cutting into that apostema many times for a long time, and successive apostemas arose in its place, which I cut into until it was prolonged.
And then I made it well and cauterized the Golauro of her, so that there was no addition; and then I walked around and did not know what God had done for me.
Chapter 37.
On the Cutting of a Hovel when it is Apostemated.
When the catarrh settles in the hovel, and it is apostemated and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is on the back part large and rounded, and then it is called a boil.
But when it is cured with the water that we have said in the division, and does not confirm your cure, and only sometimes the apostema remains of it and becomes subtle, then you should cut into that which is added on roundly; and if it reaches that length, and is sanguine, yellow-colored or black, and has no sensation to it, then you should hesitate in cutting into it, because there is fear for the patient.
And when you cut into it according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, then make the patient sit down and take hold of his tongue with an instrument of which its form is processed, and place the once in the newness, and pull it down and cut into it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the cutting of two notches.
And it is necessary that you do not cut into it unless it is adjusted to the natural term without addition, because if you cut too much, you will impair his speech.
And then use the instrument that we have recommended in the cutting of two notches, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the patient is terrified by the cutting of it, then use force in the cauterization of it without fear, and the manner of the cauterization is only with sharp water, and you pause the head of the patient in its proper place, and then grasp his tongue with the instrument that we have said, and take from the water which we have said in the chapter on cauterization, and bring to life with it, and pause it not thick or subtle, and fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
So that the extremity of it is in the concavity where the medicine is paused, just like the concavity of a spoon, and pause the instrument with the medicine on the vulva, and the patient will stretch out his arm over his own lap so that he can spit saliva or mucus from the mouth of that one because of the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend to any cause on the Golauro of him and that he may recover.
And then take your mouth and the medicine, and express it over the uvula for the space of about an hour until you see that one being darkened and soothed by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the extremity of the ray, and submerge the cloth in the medicine, and introduce the ray with the cloth into a tube above until the cloth adheres to that tube, and do this many times until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then let it be quar it will dry out and fall off within three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and then cauterize with a gentle touch in the circuit of the uvula wrapped in cloth, and purify with that what is in the circuit of it with the medicine, and be washed with vinegar water, and be cured externally with embrocation, and internally with gargling until it is healed.
And this type of cure is more saved than cutting and more long-lasting in fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with water that is more subtle than cauterizing and cutting, because it is cured through subfumigation in this manner: take calament, yseop, origan, ruda, Arabic sycamore, camomile, and similar herbs to those of plants, and adjust all or some of them into a bath, and let them be submerged in vinegar water, and boil; and the bath should be well stirred with a decent stirrer, and there should be a hole in the middle of the bath over which is placed an instrument of iron or silver according to this manner.
And the extremity should be introduced into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor can reach the uvula on the tongue, until the uvula is quite obscured.
And then repeat it many times until it is dry, and be careful not to do this cure at the beginning of the appearance of apostemas, because it would adjust too much in the apostema, and you should not do that, but rather wait for the declaration of the apostema of it first.
But if you don't have this instrument, then take a reed and shape it into the extremity of it a seal of clay, so that the mouth of the patient is closed, because the seal of clay prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Chapter 38.
On the Extraction of Thorns and that which adheres in the Golauro and other causes.
Many times thorns or spines of flesh or some other cause adhere to the Golauro.
Then you must extract that which is apparent from those thorns or spines of flesh or some other cause, on which the sight falls, because you grasp the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be declared to you what is in the Golauro.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the Golauro, it is necessary to make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because then the attached cause of the Golauro will be forgotten through vomiting; or that the patient drinks a glass of vinegar or of radish juice or a small cup of dry bread; or takes a sponge from the sea and binds it tightly with thread, and then makes the patient swallow it, so that when it reaches the place where the thorns are and is extracted, then they will be forgotten. And also take an eel and bind it with thread, and then place it inside and slowly extract it; or take a horn and then place it and then remove it.
But if the patient is not forgotten of those causes which we have said, then take an instrument of lead according to this form:
And be more thick than a ray, a little bit, and at the extremity of it be a concavity which you will introduce into the patient's Golauro with ease.
And it will raise its head up high and be careful that the touch of its bone in the throat is not felt by the patient, so that he does not feel pain or discomfort from the bone or spine, or that it touches his hand.
But meanwhile the patient that enters is mine, by virtue of my knowledge of the location of the attached cause and its descent into the lower part, and pulls his hand with the instrument towards me, all according to the quantity which has been prepared beforehand until it is forgotten.
Chapter 39.
On the Extraction of Bloody Discharge attached to the Golauro.
When the bloody discharge is medicated with the water that we have said in the division of cure by medicines and it does not confirm, consider then in the Golauro of the patient at sunset, because you have grasped the tongue of him with an instrument which we have recommended to you.
Then if you find the bloody discharge, extract it with a small once or with subtle gestures decently.
And if it is not possible to extract it, then take a concave tube and introduce it into the Golauro of the patient, and then pause in the concavity of the tube a heated iron, and do this many times (until the bloody discharge is dead and falls out of the Golauro) and abstain from giving the patient any water all day.
And then take a full vial of vinegar water, and wash the mouth of him with it, and wash his mouth with that; and do not swallow any cause or spit out the water for an hour after washing his hand, because the bloody discharge will soon come out as soon as it senses the water.
But if it is not forgotten of what we have recommended, then subfumigate the patient with cinnamon or assafetida through the instrument which we have recommended for the suffumigation of the uvula.
Do this, and it will come to pass.
And this is another manner of operation in suffumigation of that, namely: you take a bath where the coals are burning and the bath is covered with a covering in the middle of which is a hole; and in that hole, shape the extremity of the instrument; and then apply the subfumigation; and place the patient's mouth on the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the suffumigation is not forgotten until you know that the suffumigation has reached the bloody discharge, for the bloody discharge will soon come out.
And if it does not come out, then you iterate the suffumigation many times, and abstain from giving the patient water to drink, and make him eat salty causes and herbs, and do not give him vinegar water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be forgotten.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the bloody discharge is drawn from the Golauro when it is seen; and it resembles forceps in shape, such as tweezers, but with some curvature. which is placed in the Golauro, and the extremity of it resembles the beak of a stork, which is curved, with a serration of lime, so that when you grasp any cause, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the sermon in warning about apostemas and their section.
The apostemas are many species, of many manners, according to the remembrance of them, each one in this book.
And thus diverse in their perforation and section by two manners, of which one is by the species of apostemas itself, and because we grasp the humors.
And the second manner is proper to the places in which they occur in the body, because the apostema that occurs in the head is another from the apostema that occurs in the buttocks; and the apostema that occurs in a bony location is another from the apostema that occurs at a joint; and each of them has its own judgment by operation.
And besides, some apostemas are such that they must not be perforated, but rather allowed to mature in those places or complete their course.
And some apostemas are such that they must be pierced when they are crossed, and not allowed to mature according to their natural course, just like the apostemas that are proper to joints. For an apostema when it comes near a joint is prolonged within the circuit of that joint until it corrupts the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing a contraction of that member; or it is an apostema proper to a main limb, and if you delay the perforation of it until it becomes madura for the main limb; or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it before it becomes madura; and do not pierce it when it is already crossed, but only that which is proper to the anus, so that it does not trouble the prepuce and be elongated towards the parts outside of the anus, and become a fistula, or that it becomes in the end what is not healed. But it is necessary to know the hour of the perforation of the apostema, and that is when it has matured.
And you know that it is mature when there is the abatement of pain and the cessation of fever, and then the decrease in rigor and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the apostema, and other signs.
And therefore it is necessary to know that the perforation should be done in the place most distant from the apostema and most near to the cure of the Eminence.
And the perforation should be done through the length of the flesh or the body, if the apostemas are in the parts of the arms or legs, and in the location of the lacertz, cords of nerves, arteries; and, finally, in all locations where there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
And when the apostema is in a bony location, then it is well that you do not pierce it before the digestion of it has been confirmed according to its natural course, just as we have said; because if you pierce it too early, the body of the abscess will be prolonged and many unpleasant consequences will follow, and indeed the lips of the wound will be thickened and its depth increased.
But some apostemas should be pierced according to the latitude of the flesh or body, as necessity demands, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary to administer a small perforation in some apostemas, and a large one in great apostemas, with wide or many sections according to the diversity of the size of the apostemas.
And others are the apostemas in which it is necessary to have the cord cut and tied off when it becomes as if bread and butter, and when it reaches a term that has already stood, just like those that occur in many cases of dubelics and bubos.
And of these there are some that are cut off with a section having three angles; and of these, there is one that is cut like the figure of a myrtle leaf, just as those apostemas of the Angonari.
And among them are some in which a round section and a lunar section are administered, and similar to those.
But as for the one that is not like the superficial apostemas of leons, you should pierce it with a superficial or flat perforation, only so much.
And it is necessary when the apostema is large and has collected much pus, to pierce it and not wait until you have extracted all the pus at once; having removed one part, and then covering the apostema with another day, and then removing a larger part, and then doing this many times according to graduation until it is forgotten.
And most especially if the patient is of a delicate virtue, or woman in childbirth, or young child, or old person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit many times is resolved by the exit of the pus, and soon the patient dies, and you open them.
Therefore keep this chapter with great caution, and properly administer the perforation in which you pierce these apostemas, so that you clean off the plagues and consider then whether the plague of the apostema is small or is a flat section, then you should administer some of the old or fresh remedy; and if the apostema is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then it is necessary that you pause in each opening until they come together.
And if in the apostema an part of the cord is abscessed or has been concaved in the circuit, then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth or a piece of fabric soaked in hot water, just as when you treat pans or razors, so that their moisture is eliminated; and strengthen it within three days; and then process it and cure it with whatever remedy is needed for the swelling until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during the time of your operation, then you should administer vinegar water and vinegar in which a cloth is soaked, and place that on the location of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer a remedy and cure, and powder those which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the apostema is near many nerves, then it is necessary that you envelop powders in wine and hot oil, and place those on the locations.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then it is necessary that you place powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be applied three times a day, just as we have said, and then it is necessary to remove the apostema and cleanse it, and administer the remedy as needed until it is healed.
And thus is this what is necessary for the knowledge of the cure of apostemas universally, but according to the method of distinction which I have already said, each apostema in its own way is treated by specific chapters.
Chapter 41.
On the section of apostemas which come in the cord of the head.
In the cord of the head, small apostemas, and species of knots which contain tunics that are made to their own uses; and the species of them are many.
And among them are pustules, and those that contain humidity similar to lime. And among them are those that contain humidity similar to powder, and resembling their own kind; and those that are lithoid, hard.
And in the section of all and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of those does not cause contrariety to the arteries.
And the operation in section of those is that you express them with an instrument, the form of which is similar to this, which is called "entromes", until you know what they contain.
So if that which contains is humidity, then you should divide it by section according to its length, with a superficial section in this form:
And begins from the section of the line .B. to the line .G. Therefore, when the humidity is evacuated, then you should excoriate that part which contains that humidity, and do not leave any cause remaining, for many times it returns if some cause remains.
And then submerge the cloth in Egyptian ointment, if present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill it with that which fills the wound and leave it for another day, since in this way you will corrode all that remains of that peel.
And then repeat the process over that cloth with Egyptian ointment, second and third time if needed, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that peel; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the apostema contains fatty knots, then you should incise it according to this form: with a section having the form of a cross, and cut once between two lips of the section and scrape off all that part, and exert your virtue in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if your arteries make opposition, then do what I have told you first.
And similarly do in the lithoid apostema of section and cure according to what we have said; and the section on the lithoid apostema is less laborious, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these apostemas that are in the head of certain men, and which have no moisture; the cause of which is because you had incised on an apostema in the head of an old woman and then poked the apostema with a spongy, ashy, white substance that could not be bent, and if anyone touched it with that, it would wound.
But in other apostemas that occur on the head, this is not the same chapter, just as those that come to the heads of children and cause hair loss, but the section of all these should be a superficial section.
And pause the perforation of those at the lower part of them, so that the body of the pus may be alleviated in the lower parts.
And then cure those with what is needed for that of curing.
Chapter 42.
On the section of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Many times these apostemas appear in the neck and armpits and groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, and many that are generated between them, and all of this scrofula is surrounded by its own tunic, and just as with the nodes and apostemas of the head, just as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, because some are lithoid, and some that contain humidity, and some that are malignant and do not submit to cure.
Therefore, in that one which you consider to be of good disposition in the Tocament, whose color is manifest and is akin to the color of the cord, and is spread out over all parts, and is not annexed to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the arteries, and is not swollen, it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial section from the top part of the body down to the line .G. up to the line .B., and scrape off all that part, and extend both lips of the cord with one or many strokes if needed, just as we have said in the apostemas of the head, and extract that little by little.
And it is more cautionary not to incise the vein or nerve.
And if the spatula is not well adapted to your hand and incision, or if you disturb the patient, or if you incise something that is not necessary to be incised; for if you incise a vein or artery and then prohibit yourself from performing your operation, then stop in the wound's triad of wounds or other powders which prevent bleeding, strengthen the wound, and leave it until the apostema's sharpness subsides and the wound becomes softened and begins to heal. When the blood is abscessed.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire about the sign that your teacher said would indicate if there is any remaining cause of other small scrofulas; therefore, incise those and clean them.
And if it is in the root of the great vein, then do not incise that scrofula from its roots. But it is necessary to leave that alone, and not touch it with a doubled force, and let it fall of its own accord without causing harm.
And then fill the wound with old cloth that you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure it.
And if you incise that scrofula entirely, then it is necessary to adjust the two lips of the wound and cauterize them as soon as possible so that there remains no superfluity.
But if you find that there remains some superfluity due to the greatness of the scrofula, then it is necessary to understand how to incise the root of it, and to cauterize it and administer sutures according to what has been said.
And that of the scrofulas which contain humidity, pierce it with a superficial perforation at the place where it appears to you as the location of maturity, and pause the perforation from there down to the lower part of the body, just as we have said.
And then administer the perforation with a powder and similar to those, in order that it may corrode what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, cure it with an ointment of genital flesh until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
On the section of the pith of the apostema that occurs in the groin area.
They called this section in the epilhot, and it is not seen by any man on our earth that has made it.
And this is the inscription of the sermon of those.
But in men with hemorrhoids, it is necessary to avoid the extraction of the epilhot, since through this, there will be no benefit for that reason, because all the veins and major vessels of the malady are here.
But in those who have apostemas in the mouth or groin or two armpits, when the malady is not in the cane, it is necessary to use the section of epilhot to escape the destruction which is made by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, it is necessary to incise the epilhot behind three circles of the circles of the epilhot, or four with a small section for latitude in that which is between two circles by quantity, so that the section is in ciphac, not in cartilage, and this location is suitable for the section, because it is free from flesh and the vessels of blood are nearby.
And if the doctor is cautious, it is necessary then that he extends the cord of the groin with one stroke, and appropriates the cut to the canal, but avoids the vessels of blood if he sees any cause.
And then incise the ciphac according to what we have recounted; and it is signified on the section of the canal by the flame which is from that place and is broken up with water and for the abscission of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the hour is returned to which perforation is feared, add two lips of the cord to the wound, and cauterize it alone without touching the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that make the flesh heal until it is healthy.
And thus says Albucasis that the summary of the sermon which we have recounted is an exposition only to see that some of those apostemas have already filled up the groin area of the malady, and the malady has come to a point of death, and the ale of life has been extinguished, and some will go to incise the epigloss so that the malady may breathe over the site of the wound through some respiration, and thus be saved from death.
And therefore I command to leave the wound open until the malice of the malady is consumed; and it is a malice that lasts for three days; and thus I command to close the wound, and cure it until it is healthy.
And what you have seen through me is that a servant took a cote and placed it in his groin area and incised with it a part of the cane of the lung, and I was called to the cure of him, and he began to cough as if it were the dying man's last breath, and uncovered the wound and began to bleed little blood from the wound.
Therefore you were confessed that it had not incised a vein nor guided, and the breath was coming from the wound, and cured it until it was healed.
And did not happen to the servant except that he regained his voice, and then some days later returned to a state of better disposition.
And now I say to you that in the section of the epilhot there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
On the section about an apostema that occurs in the groin area outside and is called a hernia of the groin.
This apostema is called a hernia of the groin and it is a large apostema according to the color of the body, and it is often found in women.
And it is according to two manners, whether it is natural or accidental.
And in the natural one there is no need for cure.
And the accidental one is according to two manners of which one is similar to grassy nodes and the other manner is similar to an apostema made by the constriction of an artery, and in that there is fear. And it is not necessary to make an assumption about it as iron except in that which is small. And you should proceed to investigate that and examine it according to what has been said, and if you find it similar to grassy nodes, and it is not suspended from any of the veins, then you should incise it as with the nodes, and extract that which contains it, along with the skin and bursa; and otherwise, you should thoroughly investigate all its being, and then cure the location where it requires curing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the section of the species of nodes.
Of the nodes, many species and you have already said all their species in the division.
And it is necessary that you recount thus the difference between a node and an exitura, as it causes doubt.
Therefore I say that in an exitura there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity is cooled down; and it is completed putrefaction which then ceases the fever, pain, and heat.
But in a node there is no heat or fever or pain, and it contains that ciphac lion which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a cheese and it is made thus as if from a vacuum, whether major or minor, and they are according to two species, either fatty and containing humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, according to what you said in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the cure of a node, it is necessary that you expose it and inquire about it from the beginning with an instrument which is called an intro-mes, according to what will come in the chapter which is approaching this.
And this is the manner of inquiring about apostemas and all nodes, that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid location which you find in the apostema, and you turn those said parts with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the cord.
And then withdraw your hand according to the measure of the greatness of the apostema, and then extract the intro-mes and consider what is situated in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity is forgotten, of whatever color it may be, then you should incise it with a superficial section according to what I have said in other apostemas.
And if from the wake of the intro-mes there is no humidity, then you should know that it is fat.
And dry it over it with a section having the form of a cross according to this form: just as you are taught, and suspend it with an onyx, and excise the cord of all parts with ease, and preserve the skin or sac, if possible, over that and extract the pus from the node.
But if it is broken in the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and many times it happens that way, and then you should extract it by force until there remains no cause from it.
For if there remains no cause from it, either little or much, the node will return to its original state.
And if you find that there remains a small part from it, then complete the wound with some sharp, corrosive powders and strengthen the wound, and pause over that which seats the apostema cautiously, and cure that other way until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you should cauterize the two lips of it and cure that with what enfles.
But if the pulse is absent from it, or not pulsating, then you should compress and humple the site of the wound which is wet and leave that straight for two or three days until the wound is cleansed and the ebullition of blood subsides; and then return to incising what remains of the node.

Chapter 47.
On the cure of the breasts of men which are similar to those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become inflated as they begin to be full-bodied, and accordingly, the breasts are swollen, and similar to those of women, and resembling apostemes, distant.
And to him who has this condition, it is necessary that you incise those with a lunar section according to this form, from the line of B to the line of G, and accordingly, excise the tumor entirely, and accordingly, fill the wound with a medicinal flesh-coloring, and accordingly, adjust the two lips of the wound with stitching, and cure that until it is healed.
But if the breast declines into its divisions and is softened by its magnitude, just as it does in women, then let it be cut into two halves along their natural lines, with two sections resembling a lunar figure, of which each one is contained within the other, until the major line contains the minor one according to this form from the line of B to the line of G. And accordingly, excise the cord that lies between the two lips of the wound, and stop the bleeding, and administer what we have said about suturing and those powders which are necessary for the cure until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of that which needs to be cut for the restlessness of the patient and the flow of blood, then let it be filled with a poultice submerged in corrosive gallnut and leave it until the corroding liquid that is left from the grayish matter is gone, and accordingly, cure that until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On the perforation of apostemas that come forth through the navel.
Those apostemas that come forth through the navel are of the species of scrophulas, hard ones, which contain a sack.
And of those, some contain humidity; and those that contain humidity must be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from the line of G to the line of B. And when all the discharge of blood is evacuated from the apostema, then you should fill it with a poultice and leave it until the next day.
And accordingly, the poultice should be stopped and cured with a suitable dressing for that wound, until it is healed.
And if the apostema is of the species of nodes or scrophulas, then you should incise it on top just as we have said in the section about scrophulas equally.
And if the wound is extended in such a way that it would be a bother to poultice, then you should administer in it a cauterization according to what we have said before.
Chapter 49.
On the section of apostemas that occurs through artery or vein.
When an artery is broken and the cord that lies over it is consolidated, then many times an apostema occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and apostema are through the artery or vein: for when the apostema is through the artery it is long and swollen in the groin of the body; and when you impel the apostema with those said parts, you will feel a stridor in it.
And that which is through the vein is swollen around the visible part of the body.
And the section on this apostema is an error, and more so in those that are located near the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many other parts of the body; and it is very great, therefore it is necessary to avoid its cure with iron, and that which is in the extremities or at the head should be avoided.
But in that which is caused by inflammation of the orifice of the artery, incise a section on the cord in the long direction; and accordingly, cut the section with scissors and accordingly, open up the artery, and deliver the tunics until it is uncovered.
And accordingly, tie on top of that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and strengthen the artery with double thread in two places,  which cause is necessary for you to sew up two arteries which are in the temples.
And accordingly, push the place that is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood that is in it is let out and the apostema is dissolved.
And accordingly, administer the cure in which power is generated until the ligaments fall off; and accordingly, cure that with suitable dressings for that wound until it is healed.
And if it is an apostema caused by section of the vein, then you must take with your hand as much water as is possible from the apostema and the cord, and accordingly, insert the needle below the place that you have held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and be in that double thread until you extract that from the other side, and accordingly, tie the apostema with two ligaments just as we have said to you about the ligament of grapes for two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will break, then accordingly insert a needle with a thread under the entire apostema, and make the first incision of the needle, and strengthen those threads in four places.
And accordingly, cut the apostema in the middle of it so that when you have extracted what is inside it, then you should incise the excess cord and leave what remains tied.
And accordingly, pause over the poultice which you have submerged in wine and oil, and accordingly, administer the cure which is made with some and dressings until it is healed.
Chapter 50: On the apostema that occurs due to twisting of nerves.
Just as the apostema occurs in artery and vein, and similarly the apostema occurs in nerves when it occurs in their percussion or twisting, and due to excessive strain, and for similar reasons.
And it is made in the most part of the body in the locations of the joints or the ankle, or in any place where the articulations are faulty.
And it is a grave apostema, and it is similar in color to the rest of the body, and it is located in the major parts of the body, disposed in its own way, when it is tightened with vehemence, as if the patient were in a state of stupor.
And it is not an apostema that grows in the groin of the body, and therefore it is left behind the cord, and it is faulty to all parts and does not go to the parts before or behind.
Therefore, in those that are in the joints, it is not necessary to be treated with iron, because it would very easily cause contraction.
But that which is in the head or forehead should have the cord removed with a spatula. But if it is an apostema of little size, then take away that part with forceps and incise that part to its roots.
And if it is large, then you should suspend that part with thread, and excise that part, and accordingly, stop that bleeding; and adjust the wound with stitches, and cure that until it is healed.

Chapter 52.
On the cure of a swelling at the navel.
A swelling at the navel is caused by many reasons; or by fissures of the cicatrix which is above the belly, and the yarn or budgel comes out according to the way it has been torn in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery, as has been said before, or by a constricted ventrality in that place.

But if it is caused by humidity, then there is no pain and it is not hidden when you press with your hand, nor is it constricted or creased.
And if it is caused by blood, then accordingly the apostema appears with these signs declining towards blackness.
But if it is caused by nothing but flesh, then accordingly the apostema is a serious one, large and hard, and is fixed according to a quantity.
But if it is caused by a constriction, then accordingly the touch of that part is painful.
And the operation in that case is that you consider whether the swelling at the navel is caused by blood from an artery, or a vein, or constriction; then it is necessary to be prohibited from curing that, because there is fear and dread thus as I have taught in the chapter on apostemas caused by arteries and veins.
And if the swelling at the navel is caused by the budel or yarn, then it is necessary to command the patient to take hold of his own hand and be standing upright; and accordingly, make a sign with the thread in the circuit of all the navel, and accordingly, command him to stand between your two hands on his shoulders, and accordingly, circulate around the navel with a spatula in the location which I have marked with the thread; and accordingly, extend most of the apostema to the upper parts with strong thread or cede, and tie it in the circuit with a firm thread or ceda, and do so decently.
And accordingly, take the middle of the extended apostema and insert the said thread into that indication and guide the yarn; and if you have not found nor guarded it by the thread, then accordingly, extract the rest and push the yarn to the lower parts of the belly.
And if you have found the thread, then take that with a thread; and incise the excess parts of it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then accordingly circle around that part, and accordingly return to your operation; and take two needles and insert them into those two strong threads and enter the two needles in the circuit which you have made around the apostema in a cross-like manner, making them penetrate; and accordingly strengthen the apostema in four parts above the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the flesh extended and falls off by itself; or incise that part when it is softened and becomes pliable, and accordingly cure that with water as needed, until it is healed.
And if the swelling at the navel is of flesh only or due to humidity, then it is necessary that you conquer the apostema as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find in it, and accordingly cure that with water as needed, and consolidate the place.

Chapter LXI.
On the Extraction of Pain from Women.
Pain in women is rarely generated.

Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for a reason it was ordained to me that I should remember of it in this book; and it is not necessary to recall it except by two methods, one of which is because of the science of the doctor when he is asked about it, so that he knows the cure of it which occurs; and the other method is that we have many times had need of castration of other beasts for our benefits through those animals, just as with horses, and oxen, and pigs, and similar to animal-like creatures.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by castration, or by incision at section.
But in that which is done by castration, the way of the operation is that we make the animal lie down in cold water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang. And accordingly, take those with your right hand until they are resolved and do not appear to be left behind the touchment.
